The Observer Tuesday, October 30, 1984 -page 2 





In Brief 
The librarian of the Supreme Courtof 
the United States, Roger Jacobs, has been appointed director of re­search and librarian at the Notre Dame Law School by Provost Timothy O'Meara, effective July I. Jacob's appointment is one of a series of steps in the University's plan to convert its present law library to a major law research facility. Before joining the staffofthe Supreme Court in I978, Jacobs was law librarian at three different academic law libraries. He is a member of the Michigan bar. -The Observer 
The Shaheen-Mestrovic Park in front of 
O'Shaughnessy Hall was dedicated Oct. 24 with a blessing by Father Theodore Hesburgh, University president. The area is a benefaction ofMr. And Mrs. Eli Shaheen ofSturgis, Mich. They donated SI00,000 to construct the mini-park and conversation area on the west side of O'Shaughnessy, with the artist Mestrovic's "Christ and the Samaritan Woman at the Well" as its focal point and including his statues ofSt. 
Luke and ofSt. John.-The Observer 
"The Gender Gap," a movie explaining the differ­ence between voting patterns of men and women, will be shown tonight at 7 in the Centerfor Social Concerns auditorium. The movie is sponsered by the Women's International League for Peace and Freedom and the Notre Dame Women's Caucus. -The Observer 
The teacher chosen to fly aboard the space shuttle in I986 will need at leaSt five years of teaching experience, normal blood pressure and good hearing, a NASA official in Huntsville, Ala. says. Alan Ladwig, director of NASA's Space Flight Participation Pro­gram, said applications from interested elementary and secondary teachers will be accepted from Dec. I to Feb. I. The winner, chosen from a field of 10 finalists picked on July 4, I985, will become the first person drawn from the general public to besent into space. -AP 
Of Interest 
A debate between Mike Brogioli, chairman of Notre Dame students for Mondale-Ferraro, and Bill Healy, chairman of the ND/SMC students for Reagan-Bush will be held tonight from 8:30 to 10 in the Hayes-Healy Auditorium. The Election Task Force is spon­soring the debate and Observer Editor-in-Chief Bob Vonderheide will be the moderator. -The Observer 
Looking for a careerin Congress or a summer in­ternship? Gary Caruso ,director oflegislation for the subcommittee on select educations and 1973 graduate of Notre Dame, will speak about these topics tonight at 8 in the Library Auditorium. The speech is sponsored by all of the class officers and is open to the public. -The Observer 

Weather ­
Indian summer is over. Mostly cloudy today with highs in the upper 50s. A 30 percent chance of rain tonight with lows in the mid 40s. Tomorrow, a 40 percent chance ofrain with highs in the mid 50s. -AP

The Observer (USPS 599 2-4000) is published Monday through Friday and on home football Saturdays, except during exam and vacation periods. The Observer is published by the students of the University of Notre Dame and Saint Mary's College. Subscriptions may be purchased for $30 per year ( 520 per se­mester) by writing The Observer, P.O. Uninformed or uninterested? Don't waste your vote 
You've probably been hearing public service _messages on television and the radio reminding you of your civic duty to vote in next week's election. 
Maybe you shouldn't. 

The media mean well by these messages. Political types worry about sinking voter turnouts. But turnouts do not mean everything. The U.S.S.R has near-I00 per­cent turnouts all the time, but that has done little for their democracy. Much more than the percentage of citizens voting goes into the strength ofa republic. 
Your civic duty is not merely to vote. It is to become well informed about the candidates and the issues in­volved in the election and to make a conscious, consci· entious decision. 
If you are not well-informed, don't just flip a coin to decide your vote. Above all, don't make your decision based on public opinion polls. A majority of the people can very easily be wrong. The basis ofour demo(;racy is that if a majority of the people want the worse can­didate, he or she is elected. 
But don't vote for him or her just because everyone else The same is true ifa friend is. of the family's is running for 
If you feel you are well­city judge and you are plan­informed, but do not want ning on getting_ in trouble any of the candidates, don't with the law. Similarly, if vote for any of them. If·you there is only one issue you want to be on record as care about-abortion, the en­having voted, turn in your vironment, fixing the road ballot leaving blank the out to your house -and are races in which you feel you willing to subordinate all cannot vote or, if it is possi­other issues, then vote for ble in your state, write in the your candidate. At least you name of the person you have a reason for casting think would do best. He or your vote as you do. she probably won't win, but If you frankly don't give a at least you will have voted damn who wins an election, your conscience. don't vote. Don't neutralize 

Yes, it is possible that if you leave a ballot blank, someone, whether it be the Democratic machine in Chicago orIndiana's own efficient Republican machine, will complete it for you. But if you fear that, consider that they may also "correct" your vote if you vote "wrongly." 
It could be very dangerous to vote for a candidate just because you're sure of the outcome. If you like neither Reagan nor Mondale, but figure Reagan will win, don't vote for Mondale because it's "safe." If enough people did that, Mondale could pull off the election. 
But in that case, don't vote for Reagan, either, just to be "on the winning side." It wouldn't effect the outcome, but the larger the victory, the larger the im­pact it can have. 
Another way not to vote is the way your friends do. Just because no one else in the section will admit to supporting Mondale, doesn't mean you should feel you can't vote for him. There is no reason anyone should ever know. Ifyou fear ridicule, support Reagan in public·

before the election. You won't convince anyone else to vote your way, but on this campus you probably wouldn't, anyway. You need not tell anyone how you vote. Say Reagan; it matters little after the votes are cast anyway. 
It matters little what reason you use in making a deci­sion, as long as it is your own reason. If you vote for all the Italians against all the Poles because your experi­ence has been that Italians do a better job, fine. Some will disagree with you, but if your decision is made 
clearly, and you can defend 
it to yourself, go ahead.

ARRIVE WATER TOWER PLACE 

By DOUG HASLER 
News Staff 
Responses to last May's Fac­ulty/Course Evaluation are being distributed with today's Observer. 
Students received F.C.E. survey forms through campus mail last May. The results were compiled during the summer. 
A student could evaluate five courses on the survey. Each course was evaluated on the basis of six questions, five concerning the pro­fessor. 
Each professor was evaluated on his organization, lectures, concern for students, fairness, and overall ef­fectiveness. A final question asked about the workload of the course. 
Thirty percent of the students responded to the survey. In a letter to students last May, Student Body President Rob Bertino called for a student response of at least 40 per­cent to insure statistical validity of the data. 
When asked about the seemingly low response, Bertino said "We asked for 40 percent (in May) to get 30 percent." Bertino said student response increased sharply after the letter was sent out. 
Mark Rolincik, who wa.<> in charge of compiling the results of the sur­vey, described the response as "pretty much a normal response" in comparison with other universities. 
University response to the stu­dent survey ha.o; been guarded. Fa­ther Edward Malloy, associate provost, said he wants to "wait and see" how the survey Is received and used before commenting on Its value. 
With regard to the statistical validity ofthe student response, Mal­loy said "It might very well be valid. However, you just cannot know how representative it Is." 
Although both Bertino and Rolin­c1' ere satisfied with the organiza­tivll of this year's evaluations, both expect future surveys to Improve. In the ne:ln survey, they expect to save time and money by using computer sheets similar to those used in the Teacher/Course Evaluation, the University survey conducted at the end of each semester. 


SMC nurses learn in and out of classroom 
ByJOHN GORLA 
StaffReporter 
Eight in the morning is rarely a time students prefer for classes, but for Saint Mary's nursing students who are part of the clinical learning program, they are a neccesity. 
At the start of their junior year, nursing students are introduced to a 
Vonderheide apologized for last year's scanty coverage ofthe student government elections at Saint Mary's, and promised better 
coverage this year. "Saint Mary's candidates should realize we will do this," he said. "Whether of not we have the right to (endorse candi­
dates) is not the question; it Is whe­
ther or not we do it fairly, and we 
plan to." 
The editors advised the board to different style of instruction called clinical learning. Two days a week students travel to local hospitals to learn the necessary, practical skills of the nurse In addition to trying to develop a caring and compa.o;sionate attitude toward patients. 
Because the program requires the student to travel to the hospital two consecutive afternoons each week, there is very little room to add liberal arts courses to their schedules. 
In an effort to help the students juggle their schedule around clini­cal, Theresa Marcy, dean of aca­demic affairs, suggests, "Nursing students should talk with the various departments to see if they can schedule a course that will not inter­fere with clinical." 
"In courses such as Medical Et­hies," says Marcy, "the professors usually adjust the class schedule ac­cordlngly to accomodate the large

Endorsements come in for presidential race 

Associated Press 
Calling President . Reagan's domestic program "repugnant" and his diplomacy dangerous, the New York Times on Sunday endorsed Democrat Walter Mondale for presi­dent. ·The Washington Post en· dorsed Mondale "enthusiastically and without apology" yesterday. 
Reagan won endorsements Sun­day from the San Francisco Ex­aminer, which praised his "magnetic leadership," and from the Miami Herald, the Chicago Tribune, the New York Daily News, the Hartford Courant, the Omaha World-Herald, the Sunday Oklahoman in Oklahoma City, the Oregonian in Portland and the Albuquerque Journal. 
Mondale was supported by the Philadelphia Inquirer, the Detroit Free Press, the Minneapolis Star and Tribune, the Milwaukee journal and the St. Petersburg Times. 
In yesterday's editions, the Post said its endorsement of Mondale this year was in contrast with its "unenthusiastic" endorsement of then-President Carter four years ago. 
"This year we endorse Walter Mondale · enthusiastically and with· out apology," the Post said in an edi· torial. "We think Mr. Mondale · who has been maddeningly misread and mistreated by the political trendmakers this year, just as Mr.

The Post described Reagan's 
approach on arms control as "fitful 
and temperamental," and said he has 
"proved no more skillful in the 
Middle East than Jimmy Carter was 
in the Persian Gulf." And on the eco­
nomy, it said: "Mr. Reagan smiles, 
shrugs and says that high economic 
growth will close the deficit. That's 
one thing that certainly won't hap­
pen." 
lbe New York Times said in an ed· 
itorial that the Democratic 
candidate "would offer an en· 
lightened and humane conception 
of what government should, and 
,should not, do. Most ofall, he would 
bring to the White House the will to 
control nuclear weapons." 
However, the San Francisco Ex· 
aminer praised the economic 
recovery under Reagan's "magnetic 
leadership" while tying his op· 
ponent to the "double-digit inflation 
of the Carter-Mondalc administra· 
tion." 
The Examiner criticized the presi­
dent as "wrong to allow the placing 
of 'firecracker' mines in Nicaraguan 
harbors," but praised his invasion 
that "rescued Grenada ... against the 
expansion of Eastern Bloc 
totalitarianism." 
Other newspapers endorsing 
Reagan included the Baltimore 
News American, the Kansas City 
Times, the Cincinnati Post, the Hat· 
tiesburg (Miss.) American, the 
Clarion-Ledger-jackson (Miss.) 
Daily News, the New Haven (Conn.) 
Register, The State in Columbia, S.C., 
and the Greenville (S.C.) News and 
Piedmont. 
Also backing Mondale were the 
Arkansas Gazette, the St. Paul 
(Minn.) Pioneer Press-Dispatch, the 
Stamford (Conn.) Advocate, and the 
Westchester-Rockland (N.Y.) News­
papers, a group of suburban New 
York City newspapers that endorsed 
Reagan four years ago. 
Baboon transplant is working
Associated Press 
LOMA UNDA, Calif. · Baby Fae, her blood pumped by a baboon's heart, remained in critical but stable condition for a fourth day yesterday as hospital officials drew criticism for not trying to find a human donor before performing the transplant. 
The surgeons acknowledged they 
did not know the heart ofa 2-month· old human was available the day of . the operation. But a spokesman at Lorna Linda University Medical Cen· ter said It would have made no dif· ference because that heart "wouldn't fit" into the ailing Infant, who wa..'i 14 days old and hours from death when she received the 
baboon heart Friday.

The Observer Tuesday, October 30, 1984 -page 6 
Police search river for the body of kidnapped and murdered priest 
Associated Press 
WARSAW, Poland Police searched lhe Vistula River in nor­thern Poland yesterday for the body of a missing pro-Solidarity priest who lhe Interior Ministry says was abducted and possibly killed by lhree ofits officers. 
About I,000 Solidarity supporters protesting lhe abduction of Father Jerzy Popieluszko demonstrated in the soulhwestern city of Wroclaw until police dispersed lhem, wit­nesses said. But police denied the reports. 
In Warsaw, Solidarity supporters called off a planned strike at a steel factory after the outlawed union's leader Lech Walesa appealed for calm, a senior union figure said. 
An Interior Ministry communique said Popieluszko's body had not been located after divers searched two places along the Vistula River near where he was abducted on Oct. I9.

r 
Interior Minister Gen. Czeslaw

r 
r Kiszczak said a captain and two 
,. 
lieutenants of the Interior Ministry 

r have been arrested in connection 
r with the abduction, and a com­
r munique issued yesterday indicated all three had said the priest was lhrown into the Vistula. 
r 
In a nationally televised address 

Saturday, Kiszczak had said lhe cap­
tain claimed he killed Popieluszko, 
but that his statement had not been 
confirmed because all lhree officers 
gave conflicting accounts of the 
priest's fate and no body had been 
recovered. 
Popiduszko's lawyer, Edward 

Wende, said the communique was 
"significant" because, "They con­
firmed he was thrown into lhe Vis­
tula, so we can be certain he is 
dead." 
Wende said the three officers had 

been held and questioned sepa­
rately, so lheir corroborating state­
ments about throwing lhe body into 
the river was significant. 
"In lhe opinion of specialists," it said, "lhe place indicated by the kid­nappers is marked by strong water currents and whirlpools which greatly hamper the search and is dangerous for the divers." 
According to the communique, one of lhe officers said he threw Popieluszko into lhe Vistula near lhe northern city of Torun. The com­munique said the "others men­tioned a reservoir (on the river) near Wloclawek," a city about 30 miles southeast ofTorun. 
Meanwhile, a senior Solidarity fig­ure accused the aulhorities of hola ing back information. 
"I think lhe authorities will first of all play for time, hoping that the whole tension and atmosphere will subside, and eventually claim the body cannot be found," said Janusz Onyszkiewicz, Solidarity's former national spokesman. 
"This way they would avoid the embarrassing but also potentially difficult moment oflhe funeral," said Onyszkiewicz, speaking to jour­nalists Monday outside Popieluszko's Warsaw church. 
Despite witnesses' accounts lhat about I,000 people were involved in a demonstration in downtown Wroclaw and were dispersed by police, a duty officer at the Wroclaw police headquarters, contacted by telephone denied lhere was a demonstration and said "all was and is quiet." The officerspoke on condi­tion he not be identified. 
Onyszkiewicz said Solidarity ac­tivists in lhe Huta Warszawa steel mill in Warsaw called off a work stoppage planned for yesterday in response to appeals by Walesa and Warsaw Solidarity figures. 
Walesa urged supporters Sunday to avoid being provoked into "bloody revolution" by lhe abduc­tion ofPopieluszko. 


r 
r 

If Mondale wins, he'll be following
r r 
r 
r r in the steps of Harry S. Truman 
r 
Associated Press

r 
r 
WASHINGTON -If, despite the ~ polls and lhe conventional wisdom, Walter Mondale wins ...
r 
... It'll be the greatest presidential election upset since Harry S.

~ Truman beat Thomas Dewey to the surprise of just about everyone in America except Truman himself. Mondale's well aware of the anal­ogy. He keeps making it. Last week, he toured lhe farm states -a Ia Truman and said he could "smell victory in the air." Truman inherited the presidency 
r when Franklin Roosevelt died in 
r 1945 and his nearly four years in lhe White House were tumultuous -in­
r 
flation and labor troubles at home,

r 
lhe start ofthe Cold War abroad. 

r 
r Democrats at their Philadelphia r convention that summer agreed that r Truman's situation was hopeless. r Republicans had taken control of 
r 
Congress in 1946 for lhe first time in 

r 
30 years. Democratic Sen. J. William Fulbright of Arkansas even sugges­
ted lhat Truman resign. 

r Some Democrats searched for an alternative candidate; lhe popular Dwight Eisenhower refused to make himself available for a draft. "I'm just mild about Harry," said signs at lhe convention hall. 
The party split. Former Vice Presi­dent Henry Wallace, in a break with Truman's anti-communism, formed the leftish Progressive Party and ran as its presidential candidate. 

r 
Soulherners bolted at the conven­r tion over civil rights and nominated r Gov. Strom Thurmond of South r Carolina -now a senior Republican, 
then a Democrat -as lhe Dixiecrat 

r 
r 
r presidential candidate. r But Truman plunged into his r hopeless campaign with gusto. He 
whistlestopped in nearly 400 cities and towns, always introducing his wife, Bess, "lhe boss," and his daughter, Margaret, "the boss' boss." 
A supremely confident Republi­can nominee Thomas Dewey, famed as a gang-busting prosecutor and ef­ficient New York governor, wanted to do nothing to jeopardize a sure thing, so he didn't campaign as ener­getically and in his sp...eches offered windy platitudes about "national unity." 

Truman, on the other hand, went after "that do-nothing 80th Congress" at every stop. 
He was so under-financed he was sometimes cut off the radio in mid­speech for non-payment. Once in Oklahoma City, his staff had to take up a collection to get his train out of the station. His crowds were big and enthusiastic, but reporters on the train put that down to people's natu­ral desire to see a sitting president. 
On election night, almost every­one in America expected a Dewey victory. Life magazine was out with his picture on its cover labeled "Our Next President." 
Election night in that pre­computer age: Truman carries Philadelphia but by less than Roosevelt's 1944 margin. Republicans rejoice; Pennsylvania's theirs. Ditto Baltimore. Truman, at a spa 32 miles northeast of Kansas City, has a ham sandwich, a glass of buttermilk and goes to bed. 
Wallace wins enough votes in New York to deliver the state to Dewey. Massachusetts goes to Truman. Newspapers are in a dither. They had big Dewey layouts, but can't proclaim a winner. 
By 3 a.m. EST, the outcome is still in doubt. Ohio, lllinois, and Califor­nia hold the balance. Ultimately, they all go narrowly for Truman. 
And so he won -with 49.5 percent of the popular vote to Dewey's 45.1 percent. 
How'd he do it? 
Well, Truman managed to hold to­gether the old Roosevelt coalition. He carried 28 states, 23 ofwhich had vote<! for FOR every time he ran.

Sports Briefs 
Student basketball ticket applications should 
arrive in the mail this week. If you have not received yours by Thursday, contact the ticket office at the ACC. Applications and pay­ment will be collected next week according to the following schedule: seniors, Monday; juniors and studems in their ninth se­mester or higher, Tuesday; sophomores, grad and law students, Wednesday; freshmen, Thursday. Distribution will be from 3 p.m. to 8 p.m. each day. -The Observer 
The Notre Dame field hockey team was 
6-0 in games during break. The Irish outshot Western Michigan, 32­I, and won, 3-0, before going on to defeat Hope College, 3-0, with a 22-9 margin in shots on goal. With 30 seconds remaining in the second overtime period, Molly McCabe scored to lift Notre Dame over Valparaiso, 2-I. The Irish then won the Western Illinois Tourna­ment with a I-0 overtime victory against WI, a I-0 win over Southern Illinois and a 3-o triumph over North Dakota. Melissa Sommer scored six goals during the week, Corinne DiGiacomo had three and McCabe accounted for two goals. Patti ~lagher protected the Notre Dame goal in all six games and recorded the five shutouts. ­
The Observer 

The ND women's cross-country team 
competed in the North Star Conference Meet last Saturday in In­dianapolis. Since the team is only a club, the Irish could not record any points in the meet, but Julia Merkel took seventh place in the 3.I-mile race in I9:20. Other top finishers for Notre Dame were Maureen Huffer, Patty Santos, Susan Wanchow and Ann Gibson. On Oct. I9, The Irish finished fifth in the collegiate division of the Indiana State Championship Meet on the Burke Memorial Golf Course. Mary Robertson was first in that meet in I9:06, Merkel was ninth in 20:20 and Allison Cameron was 26th in 21:53. -The Observer 
The ND Women's Soccer Club will be 
meeting today at 4:30 p.m. on the Stepan fields. Any member who cannot attend should call Karen at 283-1293. -The Observer 
The ND Water Polo Club will be meeting 
tonight at 9 p.m. in the LaFortune Uttle Theatre. -The Observer

knew we had a great opportunity to win back some respectability, espe­cially on national television." 
Hilliard's run may have shifted the momentum in the Tigers' favor, but it did not last too long as the Irish offense picked up where it left off and drove the ball right down the throat of the tired LSU defense. Utilizing the passing of Steve Beuer­lein ( 16-of-28, 168 yards, two inter­ceptions) and thee running ofSmith and Pinkett, the Irish moved 67 yards in 1 S plays. Once again, the drive stalled, but, once again, Carney nailed a field goal to move the Irish ahead by more than a touchdown, 
23-14. 
The Tigers made one last serious challenge when they took advantage of a personal foul on Notre Dame to move the ball to the Irish 28, but Wickersham, under pressure from Gann, threw a pass that linebacker Mike Larkin stepped in front of and 
intercepted. 
Needless to say, the victory will quell some of the criticism of Gerry Faust and his team, at least temporar­ily. Although the win only brought the team's record to .500, it should be a big confidence-booster. 
The game also provided Notre Dame fans with a look at what they haven't seen for the first seven games this season -a consistent, solid performance over the entire four quarters. Whether things con­tinue in such a way remains to be 

Sting perform at ACC 
The Chicago Sting professional soccer team played an exhibition indoor soccer game against the Minnesota Strikers last night in the north dome of the ACC. 
The Sting won the match, which was held as a benefit for the Special Olympics, S-4, on a goal with 11 seconds remaining. 
The game-winner was scored by Pato Margetic, the long-haired player from Argentina, whom the the Sting's public address an­nouncer billed, "one of the most exciting players in American soc­cer." 
The winning goal broke a tie that had been on the scoreboard since the first half of the game. It seemed to many ofthe fans at the contest that the exhibition was set up to end just the way that it did: the "exciting" player scored with an assist from one of the crowd's favorites, West-German-

The Sting scored first in the game, but the Strikers came back to tie the score at one and again at two. Minnesota took the lead at 3-2 and 4-3, but both times the Chicago team came back to tie the game. 
The Sting played only rP.serves for most of the second half, and neither team appeared to put forth much effort. The crowd, ob­viously disappointed, responded with silent semi-attentiveness. 
With about five minutes remaining in the game, Chicago coach Willy Roy finally put his starting lineup back into the game. The public address an­nouncer urged the crowd to cheer for the stars, but too many ofthe fans felt that they had been cheated out of half of the exhibi­tion, so they remained relatively quiet. 
Iected by The Philadelphia Daily News. 
After he announced his decision, Stevenson's parents -Jack, a former Harlem Globetrotter, and Sheilah Daniels -admitted that they both had wanted their son to choose Notre Dame, although they had not let him on to that fact. 
Stevenson will sign the national­letter-of-intent on Nov. 14, the first day high schoolers are allowed to do so by the NCAA.
